					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Partially reusable launch system and space plane</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about a spacecraft system used by NASA. For space shuttles in general, see <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a>. For the spaceplane component of the Space Shuttle, see <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_orbiter" title="Space Shuttle orbiter">Space Shuttle orbiter</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="border-top:#000 2px solid; border-bottom:#000 2px solid;">
<span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/RocketSunIcon.svg/16px-RocketSunIcon.svg.png" decoding="async" href="/wiki/Portal:Spaceflight" title="Portal:Spaceflight">Spaceflight portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Spaceflight_sidebar" title="Template:Spaceflight sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Spaceflight_sidebar" title="Template talk:Spaceflight sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Spaceflight_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Spaceflight sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Space Shuttle</b> is a retired, partially <a href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Reusable launch system">reusable</a> <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbital</a> spacecraft system operated from 1981 to 2011 by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA) as part of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a>. Its official program name was <a href="/wiki/Space_Transportation_System" title="Space Transportation System">Space Transportation System</a> (STS), taken from a 1969 plan for a system of reusable spacecraft where it was the only item funded for development.<sup id="cite_ref-space_task_group_1969_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-space_task_group_1969-8">[7]</a></sup> 
</p><p>The first (<a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a>) of four orbital test flights occurred in 1981, leading to operational flights (<a href="/wiki/STS-5" title="STS-5">STS-5</a>) beginning in 1982. Five complete Space Shuttle orbiter vehicles were built and flown on a total of 135 missions from 1981 to 2011. They launched from the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> (KSC) in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. Operational missions launched numerous <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_probe" class="mw-redirect" title="Interplanetary probe">interplanetary probes</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> (HST), conducted science experiments in orbit, participated in the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle%E2%80%93Mir_program" title="Shuttle–Mir program">Shuttle-<i>Mir</i> program</a> with Russia, and participated in the construction and servicing of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS). The Space Shuttle fleet's total mission time was 1,323 days.<sup id="cite_ref-ShuttleByNumbers_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ShuttleByNumbers-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Space Shuttle components include the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_orbiter" title="Space Shuttle orbiter">Orbiter Vehicle</a> (OV) with three clustered <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne" title="Rocketdyne">Rocketdyne</a> <a href="/wiki/RS-25" title="RS-25">RS-25</a> main engines, a pair of recoverable <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">solid rocket boosters</a> (SRBs), and the expendable <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">external tank</a> (ET) containing <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a>. The Space Shuttle was <a href="/wiki/Takeoff_and_landing" title="Takeoff and landing">launched vertically</a>, like a conventional rocket, with the two SRBs operating in parallel with the orbiter's three <a href="/wiki/RS-25" title="RS-25">main engines</a>, which were fueled from the ET. The SRBs were jettisoned before the vehicle reached orbit, while the main engines continued to operate, and the ET was jettisoned after main engine cutoff and just before <a href="/wiki/Orbit_insertion" title="Orbit insertion">orbit insertion</a>, which used the orbiter's two <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Orbital_Maneuvering_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Space Shuttle Orbital Maneuvering System">Orbital Maneuvering System</a> (OMS) engines. At the conclusion of the mission, the orbiter fired its OMS to deorbit and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric reentry">reenter the atmosphere</a>. The orbiter was protected during reentry by its <a href="/wiki/Thermal_protection_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal protection system">thermal protection system</a> tiles, and it <a href="/wiki/Rocket_glider" class="mw-redirect" title="Rocket glider">glided</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplane</a> to a runway landing, usually to the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Landing_Facility" title="Shuttle Landing Facility">Shuttle Landing Facility</a> at KSC, Florida, or to <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Dry_Lake" title="Rogers Dry Lake">Rogers Dry Lake</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a>, California. If the landing occurred at Edwards, the orbiter was flown back to the KSC atop the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Carrier_Aircraft" title="Shuttle Carrier Aircraft">Shuttle Carrier Aircraft</a> (SCA), a specially modified <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a> designed to carry the shuttle above it.
</p><p>The first orbiter, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Enterprise" title="Space Shuttle Enterprise">Enterprise</a></i>, was built in 1976 and used in <a href="/wiki/Approach_and_Landing_Tests" title="Approach and Landing Tests">Approach and Landing Tests</a> (ALT), but had no orbital capability. Four fully operational orbiters were initially built: <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia">Columbia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Challenger</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery">Discovery</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i>. Of these, two were lost in mission accidents: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"><i>Challenger</i> in 1986</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster"><i>Columbia</i> in 2003</a>, with a total of 14 astronauts killed. A fifth operational (and sixth in total) orbiter, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">Endeavour</a></i>, was built in 1991 to replace <i>Challenger</i>. The three surviving operational vehicles were retired from service following <i>Atlantis</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a href="/wiki/STS-135" title="STS-135">final flight</a> on July 21, 2011. The U.S. relied on the Russian <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz spacecraft</a> to transport astronauts to the ISS from the last Shuttle flight until the launch of the <a href="/wiki/Crew_Dragon_Demo-2" title="Crew Dragon Demo-2">Crew Dragon Demo-2</a> mission in May 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc">
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_development">Design and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Design and development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_background">Historical background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the late 1930s, the German government launched the "<a href="/wiki/Amerikabomber" title="Amerikabomber">Amerikabomber</a>" project, and <a href="/wiki/Eugen_S%C3%A4nger" title="Eugen Sänger">Eugen Sanger</a>'s idea, together with mathematician <a href="/wiki/Irene_S%C3%A4nger-Bredt" title="Irene Sänger-Bredt">Irene Bredt</a>, was a winged rocket called the <a href="/wiki/Silbervogel" title="Silbervogel">Silbervogel</a> (German for "silver bird").<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> During the 1950s, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> proposed using a reusable piloted glider to perform military operations such as reconnaissance, satellite attack, and air-to-ground weapons employment. In the late 1950s, the Air Force began developing the partially reusable <a href="/wiki/Boeing_X-20_Dyna-Soar" title="Boeing X-20 Dyna-Soar">X-20 Dyna-Soar</a>. The Air Force collaborated with <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> on the Dyna-Soar and began training six pilots in June 1961. The rising costs of development and the prioritization of <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a> led to the cancellation of the Dyna-Soar program in December 1963. In addition to the Dyna-Soar, the Air Force had conducted a study in 1957 to test the feasibility of reusable boosters. This became the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Aerospaceplane" title="Aerospaceplane">aerospaceplane</a>, a fully reusable spacecraft that was never developed beyond the initial design phase in 1962–1963.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 162–163">: 162–163 </span></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the early 1950s, NASA and the Air Force collaborated on developing <a href="/wiki/Lifting_bodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Lifting bodies">lifting bodies</a> to test aircraft that primarily generated lift from their fuselages instead of wings, and tested the <a href="/wiki/NASA_M2-F1" title="NASA M2-F1">NASA M2-F1</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northrop_M2-F2" title="Northrop M2-F2">Northrop M2-F2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northrop_M2-F3" title="Northrop M2-F3">Northrop M2-F3</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northrop_HL-10" title="Northrop HL-10">Northrop HL-10</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Marietta_X-24" title="Martin Marietta X-24">Martin Marietta X-24A</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Martin_Marietta_X-24#X-24B_2" title="Martin Marietta X-24">Martin Marietta X-24B</a>. The program tested aerodynamic characteristics that would later be incorporated in design of the Space Shuttle, including unpowered landing from a high altitude and speed.<sup id="cite_ref-lifting_body_report_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lifting_body_report-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 142">: 142 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 16–18">: 16–18 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_process">Design process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design process"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_design_process" title="Space Shuttle design process">Space Shuttle design process</a></div>
<p>On September 24, 1966, as the Apollo space program neared its design completion, NASA and the Air Force released a joint study concluding that a new vehicle was required to satisfy their respective future demands and that a partially reusable system would be the most cost-effective solution.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 164">: 164 </span></sup> The head of the NASA Office of Manned Space Flight, <a href="/wiki/George_Mueller_(NASA)" class="mw-redirect" title="George Mueller (NASA)">George Mueller</a>, announced the plan for a reusable shuttle on August 10, 1968. NASA issued a <a href="/wiki/Request_for_proposal" title="Request for proposal">request for proposal</a> (RFP) for designs of the Integral Launch and Reentry Vehicle (ILRV) on October 30, 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> Rather than award a contract based upon initial proposals, NASA announced a phased approach for the Space Shuttle contracting and development; Phase A was a request for studies completed by competing aerospace companies, Phase B was a competition between two contractors for a specific contract, Phase C involved designing the details of the spacecraft components, and Phase D was the production of the spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-lindroos_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lindroos-16">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19–22">: 19–22 </span></sup>
</p><p>In December 1968, NASA created the Space Shuttle Task Group to determine the optimal design for a reusable spacecraft, and issued study contracts to <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics" title="General Dynamics">General Dynamics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Corporation" title="Lockheed Corporation">Lockheed</a>, <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas" title="McDonnell Douglas">McDonnell Douglas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_American_Rockwell" class="mw-redirect" title="North American Rockwell">North American Rockwell</a>. In July 1969, the Space Shuttle Task Group issued a report that determined the Shuttle would support short-duration crewed missions and space station, as well as the capabilities to launch, service, and retrieve satellites. The report also created three classes of a future reusable shuttle: Class I would have a reusable orbiter mounted on expendable boosters, Class II would use multiple expendable rocket engines and a single propellant tank (stage-and-a-half), and Class III would have both a reusable orbiter and a reusable booster. In September 1969, the Space Task Group, under the leadership of Vice President <a href="/wiki/Spiro_Agnew" title="Spiro Agnew">Spiro Agnew</a>, issued a report calling for the development of a space shuttle to bring people and cargo to low Earth orbit (LEO), as well as a <a href="/wiki/Space_tug" title="Space tug">space tug</a> for transfers between orbits and the Moon, and a reusable <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion#Spacecraft" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear upper stage</a> for deep space travel.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 163–166">: 163–166 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-space_task_group_1969_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-space_task_group_1969-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the release of the Space Shuttle Task Group report, many aerospace engineers favored the Class III, fully reusable design because of perceived savings in hardware costs. <a href="/wiki/Max_Faget" class="mw-redirect" title="Max Faget">Max Faget</a>, a NASA engineer who had worked to design the <a href="/wiki/Project_Mercury" title="Project Mercury">Mercury</a> capsule, patented a design for a two-stage fully recoverable system with a straight-winged orbiter mounted on a larger straight-winged booster.<sup id="cite_ref-faget_bio_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faget_bio-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-faget_patent_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faget_patent-18">[17]</a></sup> The Air Force Flight Dynamics Laboratory argued that a straight-wing design would not be able to withstand the high thermal and aerodynamic stresses during reentry, and would not provide the required cross-range capability. Additionally, the Air Force required a larger payload capacity than Faget's design allowed. In January 1971, NASA and Air Force leadership decided that a reusable delta-wing orbiter mounted on an expendable propellant tank would be the optimal design for the Space Shuttle.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 166">: 166 </span></sup>
</p><p>After they established the need for a reusable, heavy-lift spacecraft, NASA and the Air Force determined the design requirements of their respective services. The Air Force expected to use the Space Shuttle to launch large satellites, and required it to be capable of lifting 29,000&nbsp;kg (65,000&nbsp;lb) to an eastward LEO or 18,000&nbsp;kg (40,000&nbsp;lb) into a <a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">polar orbit</a>. The satellite designs also required that the Space Shuttle have a 4.6 by 18&nbsp;m (15 by 60&nbsp;ft) payload bay. NASA evaluated the <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_F-1" title="Rocketdyne F-1">F-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne_J-2" title="Rocketdyne J-2">J-2</a> engines from the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(rocket_family)" title="Saturn (rocket family)">Saturn rockets</a>, and determined that they were insufficient for the requirements of the Space Shuttle; in July 1971, it issued a contract to <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne" title="Rocketdyne">Rocketdyne</a> to begin development on the <a href="/wiki/RS-25" title="RS-25">RS-25</a> engine.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 165–170">: 165–170 </span></sup>
</p><p>NASA reviewed 29 potential designs for the Space Shuttle and determined that a design with two side boosters should be used, and the boosters should be reusable to reduce costs.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 167">: 167 </span></sup> NASA and the Air Force elected to use <a href="/wiki/Solid-propellant_rocket" title="Solid-propellant rocket">solid-propellant boosters</a> because of the lower costs and the ease of refurbishing them for reuse after they landed in the ocean. In January 1972, President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> approved the Shuttle, and NASA decided on its final design in March. The development of the Space Shuttle Main Engine (SSME) remained the responsibility of Rocketdyne, and the contract was issued in July 1971, and updated SSME specifications were submitted to Rocketdyne in that&nbsp;April.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> That&nbsp;August, NASA awarded the contract to build the orbiter to North American Rockwell. In August 1973, the external tank contract to <a href="/wiki/Martin_Marietta" title="Martin Marietta">Martin Marietta</a>, and in November the solid-rocket booster contract to <a href="/wiki/Morton_Thiokol" class="mw-redirect" title="Morton Thiokol">Morton Thiokol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 170–173">: 170–173 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:566175main_columbia-opf.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The Space Shuttle Columbia under construction" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/566175main_columbia-opf.jpg/220px-566175main_columbia-opf.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/566175main_columbia-opf.jpg/330px-566175main_columbia-opf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/566175main_columbia-opf.jpg/440px-566175main_columbia-opf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2136" data-file-height="2670"></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a> undergoing installation of its ceramic tiles</figcaption></figure>
<p>On June 4, 1974, Rockwell began construction on the first orbiter, OV-101, dubbed Constitution, later to be renamed <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Enterprise" title="Space Shuttle Enterprise"><i>Enterprise</i></a>. <i>Enterprise</i> was designed as a test vehicle, and did not include engines or heat shielding. Construction was completed on September 17, 1976, and <i>Enterprise</i> was moved to the <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a> to begin testing.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 173">: 173 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-enterprise_overview_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enterprise_overview-20">[19]</a></sup> Rockwell constructed the <a href="/wiki/MPTA-098" class="mw-redirect" title="MPTA-098">Main Propulsion Test Article (MPTA)-098</a>, which was a structural truss mounted to the ET with three RS-25 engines attached. It was tested at the <a href="/wiki/Stennis_Space_Center" title="Stennis Space Center">National Space Technology Laboratory</a> (NSTL) to ensure that the engines could safely run through the launch profile.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-163">: II-163 </span></sup> Rockwell conducted mechanical and thermal stress tests on Structural Test Article (STA)-099 to determine the effects of aerodynamic and thermal stresses during launch and reentry.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: I-415">: I-415 </span></sup>
</p><p>The beginning of the development of the RS-25 Space Shuttle Main Engine was delayed for nine months while <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney">Pratt &amp; Whitney</a> challenged the contract that had been issued to Rocketdyne. The first engine was completed in March 1975, after issues with developing the first throttleable, reusable engine. During engine testing, the RS-25 experienced multiple nozzle failures, as well as broken turbine blades. Despite the problems during testing, NASA ordered the nine RS-25 engines needed for its three orbiters under construction in May 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 174–175">: 174–175 </span></sup>
</p><p>NASA experienced significant delays in the development of the Space Shuttle's <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_thermal_protection_system" title="Space Shuttle thermal protection system">thermal protection system</a>. Previous NASA spacecraft had used <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry#Ablative" title="Atmospheric entry">ablative</a> heat shields, but those could not be reused. NASA chose to use ceramic tiles for thermal protection, as the shuttle could then be constructed of lightweight <a href="/wiki/Aluminum" class="mw-redirect" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a>, and the tiles could be individually replaced as needed. Construction began on <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a> on March 27, 1975, and it was delivered to the KSC on March 25, 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 175–177">: 175–177 </span></sup> At the time of its arrival at the KSC, <i>Columbia</i> still had 6,000 of its 30,000 tiles remaining to be installed. However, many of the tiles that had been originally installed had to be replaced, requiring two years of installation before <i>Columbia</i> could fly.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 46–48">: 46–48 </span></sup>
</p><p>On January 5, 1979, NASA commissioned a second orbiter. Later that month, Rockwell began converting STA-099 to OV-099, later named <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger"><i>Challenger</i></a>. On January 29, 1979, NASA ordered two additional orbiters, OV-103 and OV-104, which were named <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery"><i>Discovery</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a>. Construction of OV-105, later named <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour"><i>Endeavour</i></a>, began in February 1982, but NASA decided to limit the Space Shuttle fleet to four orbiters in 1983. After the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">loss of <i>Challenger</i></a>, NASA resumed production of <i>Endeavour</i> in September 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 52–53">: 52–53 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">Testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Testing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:OV-101_first_flight.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Enterprise being release from the Shuttle Carrier Aircraft for the Approach and Landing Tests" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/OV-101_first_flight.jpg/220px-OV-101_first_flight.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/OV-101_first_flight.jpg/330px-OV-101_first_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/OV-101_first_flight.jpg/440px-OV-101_first_flight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3030" data-file-height="2069"></a><figcaption><i>Enterprise</i> during the <a href="/wiki/Approach_and_Landing_Tests" title="Approach and Landing Tests">Approach and Landing Tests</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The Space Shuttle Columbia launching on the first Space Shuttle mission" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg/170px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="236" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg/255px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg/340px-Space_Shuttle_Columbia_launching_cropped_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1176" data-file-height="1631"></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a> launching on <a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[b]</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>After it arrived at Edwards AFB, <i>Enterprise</i> underwent flight testing with the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Carrier_Aircraft" title="Shuttle Carrier Aircraft">Shuttle Carrier Aircraft</a>, a Boeing 747 that had been modified to carry the orbiter. In February 1977, <i>Enterprise</i> began the <a href="/wiki/Approach_and_Landing_Tests" title="Approach and Landing Tests">Approach and Landing Tests</a> (ALT) and underwent captive flights, where it remained attached to the Shuttle Carrier Aircraft for the duration of the flight. On August 12, 1977, <i>Enterprise</i> conducted its first glide test, where it detached from the Shuttle Carrier Aircraft and landed at Edwards AFB.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 173–174">: 173–174 </span></sup> After four additional flights, <i>Enterprise</i> was moved to the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> (MSFC) on March 13, 1978. <i>Enterprise</i> underwent shake tests in the Mated Vertical Ground Vibration Test, where it was attached to an external tank and solid rocket boosters, and underwent vibrations to simulate the stresses of launch. In April 1979, <i>Enterprise</i> was taken to the KSC, where it was attached to an external tank and solid rocket boosters, and moved to <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39" title="Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39">LC-39</a>. Once installed at the launch pad, the Space Shuttle was used to verify the proper positioning of the launch complex hardware. <i>Enterprise</i> was taken back to California in August 1979, and later served in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Vandenberg_AFB_Space_Launch_Complex_6" class="mw-redirect" title="Vandenberg AFB Space Launch Complex 6">SLC-6</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vandenberg_AFB" class="mw-redirect" title="Vandenberg AFB">Vandenberg AFB</a> in 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 40–41">: 40–41 </span></sup>
</p><p>On November 24, 1980, <i>Columbia</i> was mated with its external tank and solid-rocket boosters, and was moved to LC-39 on December&nbsp;29.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-22">: III-22 </span></sup> The first Space Shuttle mission, <a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a>, would be the first time NASA performed a crewed first-flight of a spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-24">: III-24 </span></sup> On April 12, 1981, the Space Shuttle launched for the first time, and was piloted by <a href="/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut)" title="John Young (astronaut)">John Young</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Crippen" title="Robert Crippen">Robert Crippen</a>. During the two-day mission, Young and Crippen tested equipment on board the shuttle, and found several of the ceramic tiles had fallen off the top side of the <i>Columbia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-into_the_black_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-into_the_black-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 277–278">: 277–278 </span></sup> NASA coordinated with the Air Force to use satellites to image the underside of <i>Columbia</i>, and determined there was no damage.<sup id="cite_ref-into_the_black_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-into_the_black-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 335–337">: 335–337 </span></sup> <i>Columbia</i> reentered the atmosphere and landed at Edwards AFB on April 14.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-24">: III-24 </span></sup>
</p><p>NASA conducted three additional test flights with <i>Columbia</i> in 1981 and 1982. On July 4, 1982, <a href="/wiki/STS-4" title="STS-4">STS-4</a>, flown by <a href="/wiki/Ken_Mattingly" title="Ken Mattingly">Ken Mattingly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hartsfield" title="Henry Hartsfield">Henry Hartsfield</a>, landed on a concrete runway at Edwards AFB. President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Reagan" title="Nancy Reagan">Nancy</a> met the crew, and delivered a speech. After STS-4, NASA declared its Space Transportation System (STS) operational.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 178–179">: 178–179 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Description"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Space Shuttle was the first operational orbital spacecraft designed for <a href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Reusable launch system">reuse</a>. Each Space Shuttle orbiter was designed for a projected lifespan of 100 launches or ten years of operational life, although this was later extended.<sup id="cite_ref-sivolella_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sivolella-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11">: 11 </span></sup> At launch, it consisted of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_orbiter" title="Space Shuttle orbiter">orbiter</a>, which contained the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Space_Shuttle_crews" title="List of Space Shuttle crews">crew</a> and payload, the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">external tank</a> (ET), and the two <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">solid rocket boosters</a> (SRBs).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 363">: 363 </span></sup>
</p><p>Responsibility for the Shuttle components was spread among multiple NASA field centers. The KSC was responsible for launch, landing, and turnaround operations for equatorial orbits (the only orbit profile actually used in the program). The U.S. Air Force at the <a href="/wiki/Vandenberg_Air_Force_Base" class="mw-redirect" title="Vandenberg Air Force Base">Vandenberg Air Force Base</a> was responsible for launch, landing, and turnaround operations for polar orbits (though this was never used). The <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a> (JSC) served as the central point for all Shuttle operations and the MSFC was responsible for the main engines, external tank, and solid rocket boosters. The <a href="/wiki/John_C._Stennis_Space_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="John C. Stennis Space Center">John C. Stennis Space Center</a> handled main engine testing, and the <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">Goddard Space Flight Center</a> managed the global tracking network.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbiter">Orbiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Orbiter"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_orbiter" title="Space Shuttle orbiter">Space Shuttle orbiter</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Shuttle_profiles.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The five Space Shuttle orbiters launching" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Shuttle_profiles.jpg/580px-Shuttle_profiles.jpg" decoding="async" width="580" height="258" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Shuttle_profiles.jpg/870px-Shuttle_profiles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Shuttle_profiles.jpg/1160px-Shuttle_profiles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2251" data-file-height="1000"></a><figcaption>Shuttle launch profiles. From left: <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia">Columbia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Challenger</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery" title="Space Shuttle Discovery">Discovery</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">Endeavour</a></i></figcaption></figure>
<p>The orbiter had design elements and capabilities of both a rocket and an aircraft to allow it to launch vertically and then land as a glider.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 365">: 365 </span></sup> Its three-part fuselage provided support for the crew compartment, cargo bay, flight surfaces, and engines. The rear of the orbiter contained the Space Shuttle Main Engines (SSME), which provided thrust during launch, as well as the Orbital Maneuvering System (OMS), which allowed the orbiter to achieve, alter, and exit its orbit once in space. Its double-<a href="/wiki/Delta_wing" title="Delta wing">delta wings</a> were 18&nbsp;m (60&nbsp;ft) long, and were swept 81° at the inner leading edge and 45° at the outer leading edge. Each wing had an inboard and outboard <a href="/wiki/Elevon" title="Elevon">elevon</a> to provide flight control during reentry, along with a flap located between the wings, below the engines to control <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_principal_axes#Transverse_axis_(pitch)" title="Aircraft principal axes">pitch</a>. The orbiter's <a href="/wiki/Vertical_stabilizer" title="Vertical stabilizer">vertical stabilizer</a> was swept backwards at 45° and contained a <a href="/wiki/Rudder#Aircraft_rudders" title="Rudder">rudder</a> that could split to act as a <a href="/wiki/Air_brake_(aeronautics)" title="Air brake (aeronautics)">speed brake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 382–389">: 382–389 </span></sup> The vertical stabilizer also contained a two-part <a href="/wiki/Drogue_parachute" title="Drogue parachute">drag parachute</a> system to slow the orbiter after landing. The orbiter used <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear#Retractable_gear" title="Landing gear">retractable landing gear</a> with a nose landing gear and two main landing gear, each containing two tires. The main landing gear contained two brake assemblies each, and the nose landing gear contained an electro-hydraulic steering mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 408–411">: 408–411 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew">Crew</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Crew"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Space Shuttle crew varied per mission. They underwent rigorous testing and training to meet the <a href="/wiki/Astronaut#NASA_candidacy_requirements" title="Astronaut">qualification requirements</a> for their roles. The crew was divided into three categories: Pilots, Mission Specialists, and Payload Specialists. Pilots were further divided into two roles: Space Shuttle Commanders and Space Shuttle Pilots.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> The test flights only had two members each, the commander and pilot, who were both qualified pilots that could fly and land the orbiter. The on-orbit operations, such as experiments, payload deployment, and EVAs, were conducted primarily by the mission specialists who were specifically trained for their intended missions and systems. Early in the Space Shuttle program, NASA flew with payload specialists, who were typically systems specialists who worked for the company paying for the payload's deployment or operations. The final payload specialist, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_B._Jarvis" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregory B. Jarvis">Gregory B. Jarvis</a>, flew on <a href="/wiki/STS-51-L" title="STS-51-L">STS-51-L</a>, and future non-pilots were designated as mission specialists. An astronaut flew as a crewed spaceflight engineer on both <a href="/wiki/STS-51-C" title="STS-51-C">STS-51-C</a> and <a href="/wiki/STS-51-J" title="STS-51-J">STS-51-J</a> to serve as a military representative for a <a href="/wiki/National_Reconnaissance_Office" title="National Reconnaissance Office">National Reconnaissance Office</a> payload. A Space Shuttle crew typically had seven astronauts, with <a href="/wiki/STS-61-A" title="STS-61-A">STS-61-A</a> flying with eight.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-21">: III-21 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_compartment">Crew compartment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Crew compartment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The crew compartment comprised three decks and was the pressurized, habitable area on all Space Shuttle missions. The flight deck consisted of two seats for the commander and pilot, as well as an additional two to four seats for crew members. The mid-deck was located below the flight deck and was where the galley and crew bunks were set up, as well as three or four crew member seats. The mid-deck contained the airlock, which could support two astronauts on an <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> (EVA), as well as access to pressurized research modules. An equipment bay was below the mid-deck, which stored environmental control and waste management systems.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 60–62">: 60–62 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 365–369">: 365–369 </span></sup>
</p><p>On the first four Shuttle missions, astronauts wore modified U.S. Air Force high-altitude full-pressure suits, which included a full-pressure helmet during ascent and descent. From the fifth flight, <a href="/wiki/STS-5" title="STS-5">STS-5</a>, until the loss of <i>Challenger</i>, the crew wore one-piece light blue <a href="/wiki/Nomex" title="Nomex">nomex</a> flight suits and partial-pressure helmets. After the <i>Challenger</i> disaster, the crew members wore the Launch Entry Suit (LES), a partial-pressure version of the high-altitude pressure suits with a helmet. In 1994, the LES was replaced by the full-pressure <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Crew_Escape_Suit" title="Advanced Crew Escape Suit">Advanced Crew Escape Suit</a> (ACES), which improved the safety of the astronauts in an emergency situation. <i>Columbia</i> originally had modified <a href="/wiki/SR-71" class="mw-redirect" title="SR-71">SR-71</a> <a href="/wiki/Zero-zero_ejection_seat" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-zero ejection seat">zero-zero ejection seats</a> installed for the <a href="/wiki/Approach_and_Landing_Tests" title="Approach and Landing Tests">ALT</a> and first four missions, but these were disabled after STS-4 and removed after <a href="/wiki/STS-9" title="STS-9">STS-9</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 370–371">: 370–371 </span></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:STSCPanel.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The view from the Atlantis cockpit while in orbit" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/STSCPanel.jpg/220px-STSCPanel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/STSCPanel.jpg/330px-STSCPanel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/STSCPanel.jpg/440px-STSCPanel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3014" data-file-height="2000"></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i> was the first Shuttle to fly with a <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a>, on <a href="/wiki/STS-101" title="STS-101">STS-101</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The flight deck was the top level of the crew compartment and contained the flight controls for the orbiter. The commander sat in the front left seat, and the pilot sat in the front right seat, with two to four additional seats set up for additional crew members. The instrument panels contained over 2,100 displays and controls, and the commander and pilot were both equipped with a <a href="/wiki/Heads-up_display" class="mw-redirect" title="Heads-up display">heads-up display</a> (HUD) and a <a href="/wiki/Joystick#Electronic_joysticks" title="Joystick">Rotational Hand Controller</a> (RHC) to <a href="/wiki/Gimbal" title="Gimbal">gimbal</a> the engines during powered flight and fly the orbiter during unpowered flight. Both seats also had <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a> controls, to allow rudder movement in flight and nose-wheel steering on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 369–372">: 369–372 </span></sup> The orbiter vehicles were originally installed with the Multifunction <a href="/wiki/Cathode-ray_tube" title="Cathode-ray tube">CRT</a> Display System (MCDS) to display and control flight information. The MCDS displayed the flight information at the commander and pilot seats, as well as at the aft seating location, and also controlled the data on the HUD. In 1998, <i>Atlantis</i> was upgraded with the Multifunction Electronic Display System (MEDS), which was a <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a> upgrade to the flight instruments that replaced the eight MCDS display units with 11 multifunction colored digital screens. MEDS was flown for the first time in May 2000 on <a href="/wiki/STS-101" title="STS-101">STS-101</a>, and the other orbiter vehicles were upgraded to it. The aft section of the flight deck contained windows looking into the payload bay, as well as an RHC to control the <a href="/wiki/Remote_Manipulator_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Remote Manipulator System">Remote Manipulator System</a> during cargo operations. Additionally, the aft flight deck had monitors for a <a href="/wiki/Closed-circuit_television" title="Closed-circuit television">closed-circuit television</a> to view the cargo bay.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 372–376">: 372–376 </span></sup>
</p><p>The mid-deck contained the crew equipment storage, sleeping area, galley, medical equipment, and hygiene stations for the crew. The crew used modular lockers to store equipment that could be scaled depending on their needs, as well as permanently installed floor compartments. The mid-deck contained a port-side hatch that the crew used for entry and exit while on Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–26–33">: II–26–33 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Airlock">Airlock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Airlock"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a> is a structure installed to allow movement between two spaces with different gas components, conditions, or pressures. Continuing on the mid-deck structure, each orbiter was originally installed with an internal airlock in the mid-deck. The internal airlock was installed as an external airlock in the payload bay on <i>Discovery</i>, <i>Atlantis</i>, and <i>Endeavour</i> to improve docking with <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> and the <a href="/wiki/ISS" class="mw-redirect" title="ISS">ISS</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Docking_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbiter Docking System">Orbiter Docking System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–26–33">: II–26–33 </span></sup> The airlock module can be fitted in the mid-bay, or connected to it but in the payload bay.<sup id="cite_ref-Haynes-STS_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haynes-STS-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 81">: 81 </span></sup> With an internal cylindrical volume of 1.60 metres (5&nbsp;feet 3&nbsp;inches) diameter and 2.11 metres (6&nbsp;feet 11&nbsp;inches) in length, it can hold two suited astronauts. It has two D-shaped hatchways 1.02&nbsp;m (40&nbsp;in) long (diameter), and 0.91&nbsp;m (36&nbsp;in) wide.<sup id="cite_ref-Haynes-STS_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haynes-STS-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 82">: 82 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_systems">Flight systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flight systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The orbiter was equipped with an <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a> system to provide information and control during atmospheric flight. Its avionics suite contained three <a href="/wiki/Microwave_scanning_beam_landing_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Microwave scanning beam landing system">microwave scanning beam landing systems</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopes</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Tactical_air_navigation_system" title="Tactical air navigation system">TACANs</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Accelerometer" title="Accelerometer">accelerometers</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Radar_altimeter" title="Radar altimeter">radar altimeters</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Barometric_altimeter" class="mw-redirect" title="Barometric altimeter">barometric altimeters</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Attitude_indicator" title="Attitude indicator">attitude indicators</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Machmeter" title="Machmeter">Mach indicators</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Aviation_transponder_interrogation_modes" title="Aviation transponder interrogation modes">Mode&nbsp;C</a> <a href="/wiki/Transponder_(aeronautics)" title="Transponder (aeronautics)">transponders</a>. During reentry, the crew deployed two <a href="/wiki/Air_data_boom" title="Air data boom">air data probes</a> once they were traveling slower than Mach 5. The orbiter had three <a href="/wiki/Inertial_measurement_unit" title="Inertial measurement unit">inertial measuring units</a> (IMU) that it used for guidance and navigation during all phases of flight. The orbiter contains two <a href="/wiki/Star_tracker" title="Star tracker">star trackers</a> to align the IMUs while in orbit. The star trackers are deployed while in orbit, and can automatically or manually align on a star. In 1991, NASA began upgrading the inertial measurement units with an <a href="/wiki/Inertial_navigation_system" title="Inertial navigation system">inertial navigation system</a> (INS), which provided more accurate location information. In 1993, NASA flew a <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a> receiver for the first time aboard <a href="/wiki/STS-51" title="STS-51">STS-51</a>. In 1997, Honeywell began developing an integrated GPS/INS to replace the IMU, INS, and TACAN systems, which first flew on <a href="/wiki/STS-118" title="STS-118">STS-118</a> in August 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 402–403">: 402–403 </span></sup>
</p><p>While in orbit, the crew primarily communicated using one of four <a href="/wiki/S_band" title="S band">S band</a> radios, which provided both voice and data communications. Two of the S&nbsp;band radios were <a href="/wiki/Phase_modulation" title="Phase modulation">phase modulation</a> <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">transceivers</a>, and could transmit and receive information. The other two S&nbsp;band radios were <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">frequency modulation</a> <a href="/wiki/Transmitter" title="Transmitter">transmitters</a> and were used to transmit data to NASA. As S&nbsp;band radios can operate only within their <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">line of sight</a>, NASA used the <a href="/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite_System" title="Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System">Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_Tracking_and_Data_Acquisition_Network" title="Spacecraft Tracking and Data Acquisition Network">Spacecraft Tracking and Data Acquisition Network</a> ground stations to communicate with the orbiter throughout its orbit. Additionally, the orbiter deployed a high-bandwidth <a href="/wiki/Ku_band" title="Ku band">K<sub>u</sub>&nbsp;band</a> radio out of the cargo bay, which could also be utilized as a rendezvous radar. The orbiter was also equipped with two <a href="/wiki/UHF" class="mw-redirect" title="UHF">UHF</a> radios for communications with <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_control" title="Air traffic control">air traffic control</a> and astronauts conducting EVA.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 403–404">: 403–404 </span></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The two computers used in the orbiter" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg/220px-Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg/330px-Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg/440px-Space_Shuttle_General_Purpose_Computer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="445"></a><figcaption>AP-101S (left) and AP-101B general purpose computers</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Space Shuttle's <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> control system was entirely reliant on its main computer, the Data Processing System (DPS). The DPS controlled the flight controls and thrusters on the orbiter, as well as the ET and SRBs during launch. The DPS consisted of five general-purpose computers (GPC), two magnetic tape mass memory units (MMUs), and the associated sensors to monitor the Space Shuttle components.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 232–233">: 232–233 </span></sup> The original GPC used was the IBM <a href="/wiki/IBM_System/4_Pi#AP-101" title="IBM System/4 Pi">AP-101B</a>, which used a separate <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">central processing unit</a> (CPU) and input/output processor (IOP), and <a href="/wiki/Non-volatile_memory" title="Non-volatile memory">non-volatile</a> <a href="/wiki/Solid-state_drive" title="Solid-state drive">solid-state memory</a>. From 1991 to 1993, the orbiter vehicles were upgraded to the AP-101S, which improved the memory and processing capabilities, and reduced the volume and weight of the computers by combining the CPU and IOP into a single unit. Four of the GPCs were loaded with the Primary Avionics Software System (PASS), which was Space Shuttle-specific software that provided control through all phases of flight. During ascent, maneuvering, reentry, and landing, the four PASS GPCs functioned identically to produce quadruple redundancy and would error check their results. In case of a software error that would cause erroneous reports from the four PASS GPCs, a fifth GPC ran the Backup Flight System, which used a different program and could control the Space Shuttle through ascent, orbit, and reentry, but could not support an entire mission. The five GPCs were separated in three separate bays within the mid-deck to provide redundancy in the event of a cooling fan failure. After achieving orbit, the crew would switch some of the GPCs functions from guidance, navigation, and control (GNC) to systems management (SM) and payload (PL) to support the operational mission.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 405–408">: 405–408 </span></sup> The Space Shuttle was not launched if its flight would run from December to January, as its flight software would have required the orbiter vehicle's computers to be reset at the year change. In 2007, NASA engineers devised a solution so Space Shuttle flights could cross the year-end boundary.<sup id="cite_ref-YERO_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YERO-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Space Shuttle missions typically brought a portable general support computer (PGSC) that could integrate with the orbiter vehicle's computers and communication suite, as well as monitor scientific and payload data. Early missions brought the <a href="/wiki/Grid_Compass" title="Grid Compass">Grid Compass</a>, one of the first laptop computers, as the PGSC, but later missions brought <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> laptops.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 408">: 408 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GRiD_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GRiD-30">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Payload_bay">Payload bay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Payload bay"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="An astronaut conducting an EVA while the Hubble Space Telescope is in the payload bay" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg/220px-Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg/330px-Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg/440px-Hubble_First_Servicing_EVA_-_GPN-2000-001085.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3011" data-file-height="3000"></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Story_Musgrave" title="Story Musgrave">Story Musgrave</a> attached to the RMS servicing the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> during <a href="/wiki/STS-61" title="STS-61">STS-61</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The payload bay comprised most of the orbiter vehicle's <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a>, and provided the cargo-carrying space for the Space Shuttle's payloads. It was 18&nbsp;m (60&nbsp;ft) long and 4.6&nbsp;m (15&nbsp;ft) wide, and could accommodate cylindrical payloads up to 4.6&nbsp;m (15&nbsp;ft) in diameter. Two payload bay doors hinged on either side of the bay, and provided a relatively airtight seal to protect payloads from heating during launch and reentry. Payloads were secured in the payload bay to the attachment points on the <a href="/wiki/Longeron" title="Longeron">longerons</a>. The payload bay doors served an additional function as radiators for the orbiter vehicle's heat, and were opened upon reaching orbit for heat rejection.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 62–64">: 62–64 </span></sup>
</p><p>The orbiter could be used in conjunction with a variety of add-on components depending on the mission. This included orbital laboratories,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-304, 319">: II-304, 319 </span></sup> boosters for launching payloads farther into space,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-326">: II-326 </span></sup> the Remote Manipulator System (RMS),<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-40">: II-40 </span></sup> and optionally the EDO pallet to extend the mission duration.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-86">: II-86 </span></sup> To limit the fuel consumption while the orbiter was docked at the ISS, the <a href="/wiki/Station-to-Shuttle_Power_Transfer_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Station-to-Shuttle Power Transfer System">Station-to-Shuttle Power Transfer System</a> (SSPTS) was developed to convert and transfer station power to the orbiter.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-87–88">: II-87–88 </span></sup> The SSPTS was first used on STS-118, and was installed on <i>Discovery</i> and <i>Endeavour</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-366–368">: III-366–368 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Remote_Manipulator_System">Remote Manipulator System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Remote Manipulator System"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canadarm" title="Canadarm">Canadarm</a></div>
<p>The Remote Manipulator System (RMS), also known as Canadarm, was a mechanical arm attached to the cargo bay. It could be used to grasp and manipulate payloads, as well as serve as a mobile platform for astronauts conducting an EVA. The RMS was built by the Canadian company <a href="/wiki/Spar_Aerospace" title="Spar Aerospace">Spar Aerospace</a> and was controlled by an astronaut inside the orbiter's flight deck using their windows and closed-circuit television. The RMS allowed for six degrees of freedom and had six joints located at three points along the arm. The original RMS could deploy or retrieve payloads up to 29,000&nbsp;kg (65,000&nbsp;lb), which was later improved to 270,000&nbsp;kg (586,000&nbsp;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 384–385">: 384–385 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacelab">Spacelab</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Spacelab"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Spacelab in the payload bay while in orbit" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg/220px-STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="223" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg/330px-STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg/440px-STS-9_Spacelab_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4095" data-file-height="4158"></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a> in orbit on <a href="/wiki/STS-9" title="STS-9">STS-9</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The Spacelab module was a European-funded pressurized laboratory that was carried within the payload bay and allowed for scientific research while in orbit. The Spacelab module contained two 2.7&nbsp;m (9&nbsp;ft) segments that were mounted in the aft end of the payload bay to maintain the center of gravity during flight. Astronauts entered the Spacelab module through a 2.7 or 5.8&nbsp;m (8.72 or 18.88&nbsp;ft) tunnel that connected to the airlock. The Spacelab equipment was primarily stored in pallets, which provided storage for both experiments as well as computer and power equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 434–435">: 434–435 </span></sup> Spacelab hardware was flown on 28 missions through 1999 and studied subjects including astronomy, microgravity, radar, and life sciences. Spacelab hardware also supported missions such as Hubble Space Telescope (HST) servicing and space station resupply. The Spacelab module was tested on STS-2 and STS-3, and the first full mission was on STS-9.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA28_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA28-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="RS-25_engines">RS-25 engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: RS-25 engines"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/RS-25" title="RS-25">RS-25</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The two engine systems at the aft-section of the orbiter" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg/220px-STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg/330px-STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg/440px-STS-133_Rendezvous_Pitch_Maneuver_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848"></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/RS-25" title="RS-25">RS-25</a> engines with the two <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Maneuvering_System" title="Orbital Maneuvering System">Orbital Maneuvering System</a> (OMS) pods during <a href="/wiki/STS-133" title="STS-133">STS-133</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Three RS-25 engines, also known as the Space Shuttle Main Engines (SSME), were mounted on the orbiter's aft fuselage in a triangular pattern. The engine nozzles could gimbal ±10.5° in pitch, and ±8.5° in <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_principal_axes#Vertical_axis_(yaw)" title="Aircraft principal axes">yaw</a> during ascent to change the direction of their thrust to steer the Shuttle. The <a href="/wiki/Titanium_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Titanium alloy">titanium alloy</a> reusable engines were independent of the orbiter vehicle and would be removed and replaced in between flights. The RS-25 is a staged-combustion cycle cryogenic engine that used liquid oxygen and hydrogen and had a higher chamber pressure than any previous liquid-fueled rocket. The original main combustion chamber operated at a maximum pressure of 226.5&nbsp;bar (3,285&nbsp;psi). The engine nozzle is 287&nbsp;cm (113&nbsp;in) tall and has an interior diameter of 229&nbsp;cm (90.3&nbsp;in). The nozzle is cooled by 1,080 interior lines carrying liquid hydrogen and is thermally protected by insulative and ablative material.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–177–183">: II–177–183 </span></sup>
</p><p>The RS-25 engines had several improvements to enhance reliability and power. During the development program, Rocketdyne determined that the engine was capable of safe reliable operation at 104% of the originally specified thrust. To keep the engine thrust values consistent with previous documentation and software, NASA kept the originally specified thrust at 100%, but had the RS-25 operate at higher thrust. RS-25 upgrade versions were denoted as Block I and Block II. 109% thrust level was achieved with the Block II engines in 2001, which reduced the chamber pressure to 207.5 bars (3,010&nbsp;psi), as it had a larger <a href="/wiki/De_Laval_nozzle" title="De Laval nozzle">throat</a> area. The normal maximum throttle was 104 percent, with 106% or 109% used for mission aborts.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 106–107">: 106–107 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_Maneuvering_System">Orbital Maneuvering System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Orbital Maneuvering System"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Orbital_Maneuvering_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Space Shuttle Orbital Maneuvering System">Space Shuttle Orbital Maneuvering System</a></div>
<p>The Orbital Maneuvering System (OMS) consisted of two aft-mounted <a href="/wiki/AJ10" title="AJ10">AJ10-190</a> engines and the associated propellant tanks. The AJ10 engines used <a href="/wiki/Monomethylhydrazine" title="Monomethylhydrazine">monomethylhydrazine</a> (MMH) oxidized by <a href="/wiki/Dinitrogen_tetroxide" title="Dinitrogen tetroxide">dinitrogen tetroxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>). The pods carried a maximum of 2,140&nbsp;kg (4,718&nbsp;lb) of MMH and 3,526&nbsp;kg (7,773&nbsp;lb) of N<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. The OMS engines were used after main engine cut-off (MECO) for orbital insertion. Throughout the flight, they were used for orbit changes, as well as the deorbit burn prior to reentry. Each OMS engine produced 27,080&nbsp;N (6,087&nbsp;lbf) of thrust, and the entire system could provide 305&nbsp;m/s (1,000&nbsp;ft/s) of <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">velocity change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–80">: II–80 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_protection_system">Thermal protection system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Thermal protection system"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_thermal_protection_system" title="Space Shuttle thermal protection system">Space Shuttle thermal protection system</a></div>
<p>The orbiter was protected from heat during reentry by the thermal protection system (TPS), a <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry#Thermal_soak" title="Atmospheric entry">thermal soaking</a> protective layer around the orbiter. In contrast with previous US spacecraft, which had used ablative heat shields, the reusability of the orbiter required a multi-use heat shield.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 72–73">: 72–73 </span></sup> During reentry, the TPS experienced temperatures up to 1,600&nbsp;°C (3,000&nbsp;°F), but had to keep the orbiter vehicle's aluminum skin temperature below 180&nbsp;°C (350&nbsp;°F). The TPS primarily consisted of four types of tiles. The nose cone and leading edges of the wings experienced temperatures above 1,300&nbsp;°C (2,300&nbsp;°F), and were protected by reinforced carbon-carbon tiles (RCC). Thicker RCC tiles were developed and installed in 1998 to prevent damage from <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">micrometeoroid and orbital debris</a>, and were further improved after RCC damage caused in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster"><i>Columbia</i> disaster</a>.
