Beginning with <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a>, the orbiter vehicles were equipped with the wing leading edge impact detection system to alert the crew to any potential damage.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–112–113">: II–112–113 </span></sup> The entire underside of the orbiter vehicle, as well as the other hottest surfaces, were protected with tiles of high-temperature reusable surface insulation, made of <a href="/wiki/Borosilicate_glass" title="Borosilicate glass">borosilicate glass</a>-coated <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">silica</a> fibers that trapped heat in air pockets and redirected it out. Areas on the upper parts of the orbiter vehicle were coated in tiles of white low-temperature reusable surface insulation with similar composition, which provided protection for temperatures below 650&nbsp;°C (1,200&nbsp;°F). The payload bay doors and parts of the upper wing surfaces were coated in reusable <a href="/wiki/Nomex" title="Nomex">Nomex</a> felt surface insulation or in <a href="/wiki/Beta_cloth" title="Beta cloth">beta cloth</a>, as the temperature there remained below 370&nbsp;°C (700&nbsp;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 395">: 395 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="External_tank">External tank</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: External tank"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_external_tank" title="Space Shuttle external tank">Space Shuttle external tank</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:External_Tank.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/External_Tank.jpg/220px-External_Tank.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/External_Tank.jpg/330px-External_Tank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/External_Tank.jpg/440px-External_Tank.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2000"></a><figcaption>The ET from <a href="/wiki/STS-115" title="STS-115">STS-115</a> after separation from the orbiter. The scorch mark near the front end of the tank is from the SRB separation motors.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Space Shuttle external tank (ET) was the largest<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2024)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> part of the rocket<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and carried the propellant for the Space Shuttle Main Engines, and connected the orbiter vehicle with the solid rocket boosters. The ET was 47&nbsp;m (153.8&nbsp;ft) tall and 8.4&nbsp;m (27.6&nbsp;ft) in diameter, and contained separate tanks for liquid oxygen and liquid hydrogen. The liquid oxygen tank was housed in the nose of the ET, and was 15&nbsp;m (49.3&nbsp;ft) tall. The liquid hydrogen tank comprised the bulk of the ET, and was 29&nbsp;m (96.7&nbsp;ft) tall. The orbiter vehicle was attached to the ET at two umbilical plates, which contained five propellant and two electrical umbilicals, and forward and aft structural attachments. The exterior of the ET was covered in orange spray-on foam to allow it to survive the heat of ascent<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 421–422">: 421–422 </span></sup> and to prevent ice formation due to the cryogenic propellants.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ET provided propellant to the Space Shuttle Main Engines from liftoff until main engine cutoff. The ET separated from the orbiter vehicle 18 seconds after engine cutoff and could be triggered automatically or manually. At the time of separation, the orbiter vehicle retracted its umbilical plates, and the umbilical cords were sealed to prevent excess propellant from venting into the orbiter vehicle. After the bolts attached at the structural attachments were sheared, the ET separated from the orbiter vehicle. At the time of separation, gaseous oxygen was vented from the nose to cause the ET to tumble, ensuring that it would break up upon reentry. The ET was the only major component of the Space Shuttle system that was not reused, and it would travel along a ballistic trajectory into the Indian or Pacific Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 422">: 422 </span></sup>
</p><p>For the first two missions, STS-1 and <a href="/wiki/STS-2" title="STS-2">STS-2</a>, the ET was covered in 270&nbsp;kg (595&nbsp;lb) of white fire-retardant latex paint to provide protection against damage from ultraviolet radiation. Further research determined that the orange foam itself was sufficiently protected, and the ET was no longer covered in latex paint beginning on STS-3.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II-210">: II-210 </span></sup> A light-weight tank (LWT) was first flown on STS-6, which reduced tank weight by 4,700&nbsp;kg (10,300&nbsp;lb). The LWT's weight was reduced by removing components from the hydrogen tank and reducing the thickness of some skin panels.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 422">: 422 </span></sup> In 1998, a super light-weight ET (SLWT) first flew on <a href="/wiki/STS-91" title="STS-91">STS-91</a>. The SLWT used the 2195 aluminum-lithium alloy, which was 40% stronger and 10% less dense than its predecessor, 2219 aluminum-lithium alloy. The SLWT weighed 3,400&nbsp;kg (7,500&nbsp;lb) less than the LWT, which allowed the Space Shuttle to deliver heavy elements to ISS's high inclination orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 423–424">: 423–424 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid_Rocket_Boosters">Solid Rocket Boosters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Solid Rocket Boosters"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Solid_Rocket_Booster" title="Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster">Space Shuttle Solid Rocket Booster</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Two Solid Rocket Boosters that are not attached to an external tank or orbiter" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg/220px-Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg/330px-Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg/440px-Two_Space_Shuttle_SRBs_on_the_Crawler_transporter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3000"></a><figcaption>Two SRBs on the mobile launcher platform prior to mating with the ET and orbiter for <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The Solid Rocket Boosters (SRB) provided 71.4% of the Space Shuttle's thrust during liftoff and ascent, and were the largest <a href="/wiki/Solid-propellant_motor" class="mw-redirect" title="Solid-propellant motor">solid-propellant motors</a> ever flown.<sup id="cite_ref-SRB_largest_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SRB_largest-6">[5]</a></sup> Each SRB was 45&nbsp;m (149.2&nbsp;ft) tall and 3.7&nbsp;m (12.2&nbsp;ft) wide, weighed 68,000&nbsp;kg (150,000&nbsp;lb), and had a steel exterior approximately 13&nbsp;mm (.5&nbsp;in) thick. The SRB's subcomponents were the solid-propellant motor, nose cone, and rocket nozzle. The solid-propellant motor comprised the majority of the SRB's structure. Its casing consisted of 11 steel sections which made up its four main segments. The nose cone housed the forward separation motors and the parachute systems that were used during recovery. The rocket nozzles could gimbal up to 8° to allow for in-flight adjustments.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 425–429">: 425–429 </span></sup>
</p><p>The rocket motors were each filled with a total 500,000&nbsp;kg (1,106,640&nbsp;lb) of solid rocket propellant (<a href="/wiki/Ammonium_perchlorate_composite_propellant" title="Ammonium perchlorate composite propellant">APCP</a>+<a href="/wiki/Polybutadiene_acrylonitrile" title="Polybutadiene acrylonitrile">PBAN</a>), and joined in the <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_Assembly_Building" title="Vehicle Assembly Building">Vehicle Assembly Building</a> (VAB) at KSC.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 425–426">: 425–426 </span></sup> In addition to providing thrust during the first stage of launch, the SRBs provided structural support for the orbiter vehicle and ET, as they were the only system that was connected to the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_launcher_platform" title="Mobile launcher platform">mobile launcher platform</a> (MLP).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 427">: 427 </span></sup> At the time of launch, the SRBs were armed at T−5&nbsp;minutes, and could only be electrically ignited once the RS-25 engines had ignited and were without issue.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 428">: 428 </span></sup> They each provided 12,500&nbsp;kN (2,800,000&nbsp;lbf) of thrust, which was later improved to 13,300&nbsp;kN (3,000,000&nbsp;lbf) beginning on <a href="/wiki/STS-8" title="STS-8">STS-8</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 425">: 425 </span></sup> After expending their fuel, the SRBs were <a href="/wiki/Jettison_(aviation)" title="Jettison (aviation)">jettisoned</a> approximately two minutes after launch at an altitude of approximately 46&nbsp;km (150,000&nbsp;ft). Following separation, they deployed drogue and main parachutes, landed in the ocean, and were recovered by the crews aboard the ships <a href="/wiki/MV_Freedom_Star" title="MV Freedom Star">MV <i>Freedom Star</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/MV_Liberty_Star" title="MV Liberty Star">MV <i>Liberty Star</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 430">: 430 </span></sup> Once they were returned to Cape Canaveral, they were cleaned and disassembled. The rocket motor, igniter, and nozzle were then shipped to Thiokol to be refurbished and reused on subsequent flights.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 124">: 124 </span></sup>
</p><p>The SRBs underwent several redesigns throughout the program's lifetime. <a href="/wiki/STS-6" title="STS-6">STS-6</a> and <a href="/wiki/STS-7" title="STS-7">STS-7</a> used SRBs 2,300&nbsp;kg (5,000&nbsp;lb) lighter due to walls that were 0.10&nbsp;mm (.004&nbsp;in) thinner, but were determined to be too thin to fly safely. Subsequent flights until <a href="/wiki/STS-26" title="STS-26">STS-26</a> used cases that were 0.076&nbsp;mm (.003&nbsp;in) thinner than the standard-weight cases, which reduced 1,800&nbsp;kg (4,000&nbsp;lb). After the <i>Challenger</i> disaster as a result of an <a href="/wiki/O-ring" title="O-ring">O-ring</a> failing at low temperature, the SRBs were redesigned to provide a constant seal regardless of the ambient temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 425–426">: 425–426 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Support_vehicles">Support vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Support vehicles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A recovery boat with a recovered Solid Rocket Booster" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg/220px-Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg/330px-Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg/440px-Freedom_Star_SRB_recovery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2008"></a><figcaption>MV <i>Freedom Star</i> towing a spent SRB (STS-133) to Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Space Shuttle's operations were supported by vehicles and infrastructure that facilitated its transportation, construction, and crew access. The <a href="/wiki/Crawler-transporter" title="Crawler-transporter">crawler-transporters</a> carried the MLP and the Space Shuttle from the VAB to the launch site.<sup id="cite_ref-crawler_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crawler-33">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Carrier_Aircraft" title="Shuttle Carrier Aircraft">Shuttle Carrier Aircraft</a> (SCA) were two modified <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747s</a> that could carry an orbiter on its back. The original SCA (N905NA) was first flown in 1975, and was used for the <a href="/wiki/Approach_and_Landing_Tests" title="Approach and Landing Tests">ALT</a> and ferrying the orbiter from Edwards AFB to the KSC on all missions prior to 1991. A second SCA (N911NA) was acquired in 1988, and was first used to transport <i>Endeavour</i> from the factory to the KSC. Following the retirement of the Space Shuttle, N905NA was put on display at the JSC, and N911NA was put on display at the <a href="/wiki/Joe_Davies_Heritage_Airpark" title="Joe Davies Heritage Airpark">Joe Davies Heritage Airpark</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palmdale,_California" title="Palmdale, California">Palmdale, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: I–377–391">: I–377–391 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-joe_davies_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-joe_davies-34">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mobile_lounge#NASA_Crew_Transport_Vehicles" title="Mobile lounge">Crew Transport Vehicle</a> (CTV) was a modified airport <a href="/wiki/Jet_bridge" title="Jet bridge">jet bridge</a> that was used to assist astronauts to egress from the orbiter after landing, where they would undergo their post-mission medical checkups.<sup id="cite_ref-ctv_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ctv-35">[33]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Astronaut_transfer_van" title="Astronaut transfer van">Astrovan</a> transported astronauts from the crew quarters in the Operations and Checkout Building to the launch pad on launch day.<sup id="cite_ref-astrovan_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-astrovan-36">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/NASA_Railroad" title="NASA Railroad">NASA Railroad</a> comprised three locomotives that transported SRB segments from the <a href="/wiki/Florida_East_Coast_Railway" title="Florida East Coast Railway">Florida East Coast Railway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Titusville,_Florida" title="Titusville, Florida">Titusville</a> to the KSC.<sup id="cite_ref-railroad_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-railroad-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_profile">Mission profile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mission profile"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_preparation">Launch preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Launch preparation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Launch_commit_criteria" title="Launch commit criteria">Launch commit criteria</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The Space Shuttle moving to the launch complex on a crawler-transporter" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg/220px-Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg/330px-Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg/440px-Crawler_transporter_with_Atlantis_on_the_ramp_to_pad_39A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2008"></a><figcaption>The crawler-transporter with <i>Atlantis</i> on the ramp to LC-39A for <a href="/wiki/STS-117" title="STS-117">STS-117</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The Space Shuttle was prepared for launch primarily in the VAB at the KSC. The SRBs were assembled and attached to the external tank on the MLP. The orbiter vehicle was prepared at the <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Processing_Facility" title="Orbiter Processing Facility">Orbiter Processing Facility</a> (OPF) and transferred to the VAB, where a crane was used to rotate it to the vertical orientation and mate it to the external tank.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 132–133">: 132–133 </span></sup> Once the entire stack was assembled, the MLP was carried for 5.6&nbsp;km (3.5&nbsp;mi) to <a href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_39" class="mw-redirect" title="Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39</a> by one of the <a href="/wiki/Crawler-transporter" title="Crawler-transporter">crawler-transporters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 137">: 137 </span></sup> After the Space Shuttle arrived at one of the two launchpads, it would connect to the Fixed and Rotation Service Structures, which provided servicing capabilities, payload insertion, and crew transportation.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 139–141">: 139–141 </span></sup> The crew was transported to the launch pad at T−3&nbsp;hours and entered the orbiter vehicle, which was closed at T−2&nbsp;hours.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–8">: III–8 </span></sup> Liquid oxygen and hydrogen were loaded into the external tank via umbilicals that attached to the orbiter vehicle, which began at T−5&nbsp;hours&nbsp;35&nbsp;minutes. At T−3&nbsp;hours&nbsp;45&nbsp;minutes, the hydrogen fast-fill was complete, followed 15&nbsp;minutes later by the oxygen tank fill. Both tanks were slowly filled up until the launch as the oxygen and hydrogen evaporated.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–186">: II–186 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Launch_commit_criteria" title="Launch commit criteria">launch commit criteria</a> considered precipitation, temperatures, cloud cover, lightning forecast, wind, and humidity.<sup id="cite_ref-weather_launch_criteria_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weather_launch_criteria-38">[36]</a></sup> The Space Shuttle was not launched under conditions where it could have been struck by <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>, as its exhaust plume could have triggered lightning by providing a current path to ground after launch, which occurred on <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12#Mission_highlights" title="Apollo 12">Apollo&nbsp;12</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-chaikin_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chaikin-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 239">: 239 </span></sup> The NASA Anvil Rule for a Shuttle launch stated that an <a href="/wiki/Anvil_cloud" class="mw-redirect" title="Anvil cloud">anvil cloud</a> could not appear within a distance of 19&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a> (10&nbsp;nmi).<sup id="cite_ref-anvil_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anvil-40">[38]</a></sup> The Shuttle Launch Weather Officer monitored conditions until the final decision to scrub a launch was announced. In addition to the weather at the launch site, conditions had to be acceptable at one of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_abort_modes#Transoceanic_abort_landing" title="Space Shuttle abort modes">Transatlantic Abort Landing sites</a> and the SRB recovery area.<sup id="cite_ref-weather_launch_criteria_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weather_launch_criteria-38">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sts121_blog_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sts121_blog-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Launch"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The Space Shuttle Main Engines igniting before liftoff" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg/260px-020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="202" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg/390px-020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg/520px-020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2329"></a><figcaption>The three RS-25 main engines immediately after ignition. The <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_plume" class="mw-redirect" title="Exhaust plume">exhaust plumes</a> are mostly transparent, but bright white <a href="/wiki/Shock_diamonds" class="mw-redirect" title="Shock diamonds">shock diamonds</a> are visible below each nozzle.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The SRBs separating during the ascent of the Space Shuttle during STS-1" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg/220px-STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="277" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg/330px-STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg/440px-STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2224" data-file-height="2799"></a><figcaption>Solid rocket booster (SRB) separation during STS-1</figcaption></figure>
<p>The mission crew and the Launch Control Center (LCC) personnel completed systems checks throughout the countdown. Two built-in holds at T−20&nbsp;minutes and T−9&nbsp;minutes provided scheduled breaks to address any issues and additional preparation.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–8">: III–8 </span></sup> After the built-in hold at T−9&nbsp;minutes, the countdown was automatically controlled by the Ground Launch Sequencer (GLS) at the LCC, which stopped the countdown if it sensed a critical problem with any of the Space Shuttle's onboard systems.<sup id="cite_ref-sts121_blog_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sts121_blog-41">[39]</a></sup> At T−3&nbsp;minutes&nbsp;45&nbsp;seconds, the engines began conducting gimbal tests, which were concluded at T−2&nbsp;minutes&nbsp;15&nbsp;seconds. The ground <a href="/wiki/Launch_Processing_System" title="Launch Processing System">Launch Processing System</a> handed off the control to the orbiter vehicle's GPCs at T−31&nbsp;seconds. At T−16&nbsp;seconds, the GPCs armed the SRBs, the sound suppression system (SPS) began to drench the MLP and SRB trenches with 1,100,000&nbsp;L (300,000&nbsp;U.S.&nbsp;gal) of water to protect the orbiter vehicle from damage by <a href="/wiki/Acoustical" class="mw-redirect" title="Acoustical">acoustical</a> energy and rocket exhaust reflected from the flame trench and MLP during lift-off.<sup id="cite_ref-sound_suppression_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sound_suppression-42">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sps_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sps-43">[41]</a></sup> At T−10&nbsp;seconds, hydrogen igniters were activated under each engine bell to quell the stagnant gas inside the cones before ignition. Failure to burn these gases could trip the onboard sensors and create the possibility of an overpressure and explosion of the vehicle during the firing phase. The hydrogen tank's prevalves were opened at T−9.5&nbsp;seconds in preparation for engine start.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–186">: II–186 </span></sup>
</p><p>Beginning at T−6.6&nbsp;seconds, the main engines were ignited sequentially at 120-millisecond intervals. All three RS-25 engines were required to reach 90% rated thrust by T−3&nbsp;seconds, otherwise the GPCs would initiate an <a href="/wiki/RSLS_Abort" class="mw-redirect" title="RSLS Abort">RSLS abort</a>. If all three engines indicated nominal performance by T−3&nbsp;seconds, they were commanded to gimbal to liftoff configuration and the command would be issued to arm the SRBs for ignition at T−0.<sup id="cite_ref-countdown101_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countdown101-44">[42]</a></sup> Between T−6.6&nbsp;seconds and T−3&nbsp;seconds, while the RS-25 engines were firing but the SRBs were still bolted to the pad, the offset thrust would cause the Space Shuttle to pitch down 650&nbsp;mm (25.5&nbsp;in) measured at the tip of the external tank; the 3-second delay allowed the stack to return to nearly vertical before SRB ignition. This movement was nicknamed the "twang." At T−0, the eight <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnic_fastener" title="Pyrotechnic fastener">frangible nuts</a> holding the SRBs to the pad were detonated, the final umbilicals were disconnected, the SSMEs were commanded to 100% throttle, and the SRBs were ignited.<sup id="cite_ref-nuts0_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nuts0-45">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frang_nut_liftoff_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frang_nut_liftoff-46">[44]</a></sup> By T+0.23&nbsp;seconds, the SRBs built up enough thrust for liftoff to commence, and reached maximum chamber pressure by T+0.6&nbsp;seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–186">: II–186 </span></sup> At T−0, the JSC <a href="/wiki/Mission_Control_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission Control Center">Mission Control Center</a> assumed control of the flight from the LCC.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–9">: III–9 </span></sup>
</p><p>At T+4&nbsp;seconds, when the Space Shuttle reached an altitude of 22 meters (73&nbsp;ft), the RS-25 engines were throttled up to 104.5%. At approximately T+7&nbsp;seconds, the Space Shuttle rolled to a heads-down orientation at an altitude of 110 meters (350&nbsp;ft), which reduced aerodynamic stress and provided an improved communication and navigation orientation. Approximately 20–30&nbsp;seconds into ascent and an altitude of 2,700 meters (9,000&nbsp;ft), the RS-25 engines were throttled down to 65–72% to reduce the maximum aerodynamic forces at <a href="/wiki/Max_Q" class="mw-redirect" title="Max Q">Max Q</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–8–9">: III–8–9 </span></sup> Additionally, the shape of the SRB propellant was designed to cause thrust to decrease at the time of Max Q.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 427">: 427 </span></sup>  The GPCs could dynamically control the throttle of the RS-25 engines based upon the performance of the SRBs.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–187">: II–187 </span></sup>
</p><p>At approximately T+123&nbsp;seconds and an altitude of 46,000 meters (150,000&nbsp;ft), pyrotechnic fasteners released the SRBs, which reached an <a href="/wiki/Apogee" class="mw-redirect" title="Apogee">apogee</a> of 67,000 meters (220,000&nbsp;ft) before parachuting into the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>. The Space Shuttle continued its ascent using only the RS-25 engines. On earlier missions, the Space Shuttle remained in the heads-down orientation to maintain communications with the <a href="/wiki/Tracking_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Tracking station">tracking station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_Island,_Bermuda" title="Cooper's Island, Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, but later missions, beginning with <a href="/wiki/STS-87" title="STS-87">STS-87</a>, rolled to a heads-up orientation at T+6&nbsp;minutes for communication with the <a href="/wiki/Tracking_and_data_relay_satellite" title="Tracking and data relay satellite">tracking and data relay satellite</a> constellation. The RS-25 engines were throttled at T+7&nbsp;minutes&nbsp;30&nbsp;seconds to limit vehicle acceleration to 3 <i>g</i>. At 6&nbsp;seconds prior to main engine cutoff (MECO), which occurred at T+8&nbsp;minutes&nbsp;30&nbsp;seconds, the RS-25 engines were throttled down to 67%. The GPCs controlled ET separation and dumped the remaining liquid oxygen and hydrogen to prevent outgassing while in orbit. The ET continued on a ballistic trajectory and broke up during reentry, with some small pieces landing in the Indian or Pacific Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–9–10">: III–9–10 </span></sup>
</p><p>Early missions used two firings of the OMS to achieve orbit; the first firing raised the apogee while the second circularized the orbit. Missions after <a href="/wiki/STS-38" title="STS-38">STS-38</a> used the RS-25 engines to achieve the optimal apogee, and used the OMS engines to circularize the orbit. The orbital altitude and inclination were mission-dependent, and the Space Shuttle's orbits varied from 220 to 620&nbsp;km (120 to 335&nbsp;nmi).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–10">: III–10 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_orbit">In orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: In orbit"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The Space Shuttle Endeavour docked with the International Space Station" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/220px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/330px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/440px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="6048"></a><figcaption><i>Endeavour</i> docked at ISS during the STS-134 mission</figcaption></figure>
<p>The type of mission the Space Shuttle was assigned to dictate the type of orbit that it entered. The initial design of the reusable Space Shuttle envisioned an increasingly cheap launch platform to deploy commercial and government satellites. Early missions routinely ferried satellites, which determined the type of orbit that the orbiter vehicle would enter. Following the <i>Challenger</i> disaster, many commercial payloads were moved to expendable commercial rockets, such as the <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–108, 123">: III–108, 123 </span></sup> While later missions still launched commercial payloads, Space Shuttle assignments were routinely directed towards scientific payloads, such as the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–148">: III–148 </span></sup> Spacelab,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_2-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 434–435">: 434–435 </span></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo spacecraft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–140">: III–140 </span></sup>  Beginning with <a href="/wiki/STS-71" title="STS-71">STS-71</a>, the orbiter vehicle conducted dockings with the <a href="/wiki/Mir_space_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Mir space station">Mir space station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–224">: III–224 </span></sup> In its final decade of operation, the Space Shuttle was used for the construction of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–264">: III–264 </span></sup> Most missions involved staying in orbit several days to two weeks, although longer missions were possible with the <a href="/wiki/Extended_Duration_Orbiter" title="Extended Duration Orbiter">Extended Duration Orbiter</a> pallet.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–86">: III–86 </span></sup> The 17 day 15 hour <a href="/wiki/STS-80" title="STS-80">STS-80</a> mission was the longest Space Shuttle mission duration.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–238">: III–238 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-entry_and_landing">Re-entry and landing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Re-entry and landing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A view of the commander and pilot during reentry on STS-42" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg/220px-Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg/330px-Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg/440px-Space_Shuttle_reentry_aboard_flight_deck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4095" data-file-height="2700"></a><figcaption>Flight deck view of <i>Discovery</i> during <a href="/wiki/STS-42" title="STS-42">STS-42</a> re-entry</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif/220px-Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif/330px-Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif/440px-Typical_Space_Shuttle_reentry_profile.gif 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="513"></a><figcaption>Typical Space Shuttle reentry profile</figcaption></figure>
<p>Approximately four hours prior to deorbit, the crew began preparing the orbiter vehicle for reentry by closing the payload doors, radiating excess heat, and retracting the Ku&nbsp;band antenna. The orbiter vehicle maneuvered to an upside-down, tail-first orientation and began a 2–4&nbsp;minute OMS burn approximately 20&nbsp;minutes before it reentered the atmosphere. The orbiter vehicle reoriented itself to a nose-forward position with a 40° angle-of-attack, and the forward <a href="/wiki/Reaction_control_system" title="Reaction control system">reaction control system</a> (RCS) jets were emptied of fuel and disabled prior to reentry. The orbiter vehicle's reentry was defined as starting at an altitude of 120&nbsp;km (400,000&nbsp;ft), when it was traveling at approximately Mach 25. The orbiter vehicle's reentry was controlled by the GPCs, which followed a preset angle-of-attack plan to prevent unsafe heating of the TPS. During reentry, the orbiter's speed was regulated by altering the amount of drag produced, which was controlled by means of angle of attack, as well as bank angle. The latter could be used to control drag without changing the angle of attack. A series of roll reversals<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[c]</a></sup> were performed to control azimuth while banking.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> The orbiter vehicle's aft RCS jets were disabled as its ailerons, elevators, and rudder became effective in the lower atmosphere. At an altitude of 46&nbsp;km (150,000&nbsp;ft), the orbiter vehicle opened its <a href="/wiki/Speed_brake" class="mw-redirect" title="Speed brake">speed brake</a> on the vertical stabilizer. At 8&nbsp;minutes&nbsp;44&nbsp;seconds prior to landing, the crew deployed the air data probes, and began lowering the angle-of-attack to 36°.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–12">: III–12 </span></sup>  The orbiter's maximum <a href="/wiki/Glide_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Glide ratio">glide ratio</a>/<a href="/wiki/Lift-to-drag_ratio" title="Lift-to-drag ratio">lift-to-drag ratio</a> varied considerably with speed, ranging from 1.3 at <a href="/wiki/Hypersonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypersonic">hypersonic</a> speeds to 4.9 at subsonic speeds.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: II–1">: II–1 </span></sup> The orbiter vehicle flew to one of the two Heading Alignment Cones, located 48&nbsp;km (30&nbsp;mi) away from each end of the runway's centerline, where it made its final turns to dissipate excess energy prior to its approach and landing. Once the orbiter vehicle was traveling subsonically, the crew took over manual control of the flight.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–13">: III–13 </span></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Discovery deployed a parachute to slow itself after landing" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg/220px-Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg/330px-Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg/440px-Space_Shuttle_Discovery_Landing_after_STS-124.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3789" data-file-height="2328"></a><figcaption><i>Discovery</i> deploying its <a href="/wiki/Drogue_parachute" title="Drogue parachute">brake parachute</a> after landing on <a href="/wiki/STS-124" title="STS-124">STS-124</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The approach and landing phase began when the orbiter vehicle was at an altitude of 3,000&nbsp;m (10,000&nbsp;ft) and traveling at 150&nbsp;m/s (300&nbsp;kn). The orbiter followed either a -20° or -18° glideslope and descended at approximately 51&nbsp;m/s (167&nbsp;ft/s). The speed brake was used to keep a continuous speed, and crew initiated a pre-flare maneuver to a -1.5° glideslope at an altitude of 610&nbsp;m (2,000&nbsp;ft). The landing gear was deployed 10&nbsp;seconds prior to touchdown, when the orbiter was at an altitude of 91&nbsp;m (300&nbsp;ft) and traveling 150&nbsp;m/s (288&nbsp;kn). A final flare maneuver reduced the orbiter vehicle's descent rate to 0.9&nbsp;m/s (3&nbsp;ft/s), with touchdown occurring at 100–150&nbsp;m/s (195–295&nbsp;kn), depending on the weight of the orbiter vehicle. After the landing gear touched down, the crew deployed a drag chute out of the vertical stabilizer, and began wheel braking when the orbiter was traveling slower than 72&nbsp;m/s (140&nbsp;kn). After the orbiter's wheels stopped, the crew deactivated the flight components and prepared to exit.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–13">: III–13 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Landing_sites">Landing sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Landing sites"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Space_Shuttle_landing_sites" title="List of Space Shuttle landing sites">List of Space Shuttle landing sites</a></div>
<p>The primary Space Shuttle landing site was the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Landing_Facility" title="Shuttle Landing Facility">Shuttle Landing Facility</a> at KSC, where 78 of the 133 successful landings occurred. In the event of unfavorable landing conditions, the Shuttle could delay its landing or land at an alternate location. The primary alternate was Edwards AFB, which was used for 54 landings.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–18–20">: III–18–20 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/STS-3" title="STS-3">STS-3</a> landed at the <a href="/wiki/White_Sands_Space_Harbor" title="White Sands Space Harbor">White Sands Space Harbor</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> and required extensive post-processing after exposure to the <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a>-rich sand, some of which was found in <i>Columbia</i> debris after <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">STS-107</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–28">: III–28 </span></sup> Landings at alternate airfields required the Shuttle Carrier Aircraft to transport the orbiter back to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–13">: III–13 </span></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the pre-planned landing airfields, there were 85 agreed-upon <a href="/wiki/Emergency_landing_sites" class="mw-redirect" title="Emergency landing sites">emergency landing sites</a> to be used in different abort scenarios, with 58 located in other countries. The landing locations were chosen based upon political relationships, favorable weather, a runway at least 2,300&nbsp;m (7,500&nbsp;ft) long, and <a href="/wiki/TACAN" class="mw-redirect" title="TACAN">TACAN</a> or <a href="/wiki/Distance_measuring_equipment" title="Distance measuring equipment">DME</a> equipment. Additionally, as the orbiter vehicle only had UHF radios, international sites with only VHF radios would have been unable to communicate directly with the crew. Facilities on the east coast of the US were planned for East Coast Abort Landings, while several sites in Europe and Africa were planned in the event of a Transoceanic Abort Landing. The facilities were prepared with equipment and personnel in the event of an emergency shuttle landing but were never used.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–19">: III–19 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-landing_processing">Post-landing processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Post-landing processing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Processing_Facility" title="Orbiter Processing Facility">Orbiter Processing Facility</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="The Space Shuttle Discovery on the runway as ground crews work to get the crew out of the orbiter" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg/220px-Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg/330px-Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg/440px-Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1898" data-file-height="1416"></a><figcaption><i>Discovery</i> being prepared after landing for crew disembarkment following <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>After the landing, ground crews approached the orbiter to conduct safety checks. Teams wearing self-contained breathing gear tested for the presence of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>, monomethylhydrazine, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_tetroxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitrogen tetroxide">nitrogen tetroxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> to ensure the landing area was safe.<sup id="cite_ref-afterlandingpao_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afterlandingpao-50">[47]</a></sup> Air conditioning and Freon lines were connected to cool the crew and equipment and dissipate excess heat from reentry.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-13">: III-13 </span></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Flight_surgeon" title="Flight surgeon">flight surgeon</a> boarded the orbiter and performed medical checks of the crew before they disembarked. Once the orbiter was secured, it was towed to the OPF to be inspected, repaired, and prepared for the next mission.<sup id="cite_ref-afterlandingpao_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afterlandingpao-50">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Shuttle_program">Space Shuttle program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Space Shuttle program"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a></div>
<p>The Space Shuttle flew from April 12, 1981,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–24">: III–24 </span></sup> until July 21, 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–398">: III–398 </span></sup>  Throughout the program, the Space Shuttle had 135 missions,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–398">: III–398 </span></sup> of which 133 returned safely.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–80, 304">: III–80, 304 </span></sup> Throughout its lifetime, the Space Shuttle was used to conduct scientific research,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–188">: III–188 </span></sup> deploy commercial,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–66">: III–66 </span></sup> military,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–68">: III–68 </span></sup> and scientific payloads,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–148">: III–148 </span></sup> and was involved in the construction and operation of <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a><sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–216">: III–216 </span></sup> and the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–264">: III–264 </span></sup> During its tenure, the Space Shuttle served as the only U.S. vehicle to launch astronauts, of which there was no replacement until the launch of <a href="/wiki/Crew_Dragon_Demo-2" title="Crew Dragon Demo-2">Crew Dragon Demo-2</a> on May 30, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-demo-2_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-demo-2-51">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Budget">Budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Budget"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The overall NASA budget of the Space Shuttle program has been estimated to be $221&nbsp;billion (in 2012 dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III−488">: III−488 </span></sup> The developers of the Space Shuttle advocated for reusability as a cost-saving measure, which resulted in higher development costs for presumed lower costs-per-launch. During the design of the Space Shuttle, the Phase B proposals were not as cheap as the initial Phase A estimates indicated; Space Shuttle program manager Robert Thompson acknowledged that reducing cost-per-pound was not the primary objective of the further design phases, as other technical requirements could not be met with the reduced costs.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III−489−490">: III−489−490 </span></sup> Development estimates made in 1972 projected a per-pound cost of payload as low as $1,109 (in 2012) per pound, but the actual payload costs, not to include the costs for the research and development of the Space Shuttle, were $37,207 (in 2012) per pound.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III−491">: III−491 </span></sup> Per-launch costs varied throughout the program and were dependent on the rate of flights as well as research, development, and investigation proceedings throughout the Space Shuttle program. In 1982, NASA published an estimate of $260&nbsp;million (in 2012) per flight, which was based on the prediction of 24 flights per year for a decade. The per-launch cost from 1995 to 2002, when the orbiters and ISS were not being constructed and there was no recovery work following a loss of crew, was $806&nbsp;million. NASA published a study in 1999 that concluded that costs were $576&nbsp;million (in 2012) if there were seven launches per year. In 2009, NASA determined that the cost of adding a single launch per year was $252&nbsp;million (in 2012), which indicated that much of the Space Shuttle program costs are for year-round personnel and operations that continued regardless of the launch rate. Accounting for the entire Space Shuttle program budget, the per-launch cost was $1.642&nbsp;billion (in 2012).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III−490">: III−490 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disasters">Disasters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Disasters"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Space Shuttle Challenger disaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">Space Shuttle Columbia disaster</a></div>
<p>On January 28, 1986, <a href="/wiki/STS-51-L" title="STS-51-L">STS-51-L</a> disintegrated 73 seconds after launch, due to the failure of the right SRB, killing all seven astronauts on board <i>Challenger</i>. The disaster was caused by the low-temperature impairment of an O-ring, a mission-critical seal used between segments of the SRB casing. Failure of the O-ring allowed hot combustion gases to escape from between the booster sections and burn through the adjacent ET, leading to a sequence of catastrophic events which caused the orbiter to disintegrate.<sup id="cite_ref-challenger_report_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-challenger_report-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 71">: 71 </span></sup> Repeated warnings from design engineers voicing concerns about the lack of evidence of the O-rings' safety when the temperature was below 53&nbsp;°F (12&nbsp;°C) had been ignored by NASA managers.<sup id="cite_ref-challenger_report_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-challenger_report-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">: 148 </span></sup>
</p><p>On February 1, 2003, <i>Columbia</i> disintegrated during re-entry, killing all seven of the <a href="/wiki/STS-107" title="STS-107">STS-107</a> crew, because of damage to the <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_carbon-carbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Reinforced carbon-carbon">carbon-carbon</a> leading edge of the wing caused during launch. Ground control engineers had made three separate requests for high-resolution images taken by the Department of Defense that would have provided an understanding of the extent of the damage, while NASA's chief TPS engineer requested that astronauts on board <i>Columbia</i> be allowed to leave the vehicle to inspect the damage. NASA managers intervened to stop the Department of Defense's imaging of the orbiter and refused the request for the spacewalk,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–323">: III–323 </span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-century_of_flight_columbia_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-century_of_flight_columbia-53">[50]</a></sup> and thus the feasibility of scenarios for astronaut repair or rescue by <i>Atlantis</i> were not considered by NASA management at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-sts-107_timeline_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sts-107_timeline-54">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Criticism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Space_Shuttle_program" title="Criticism of the Space Shuttle program">Criticism of the Space Shuttle program</a></div>
<p>The partial reusability of the Space Shuttle was one of the primary design requirements during its initial development.<sup id="cite_ref-dev_space_shuttle_12-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dev_space_shuttle-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 164">: 164 </span></sup> The technical decisions that dictated the orbiter's return and re-use reduced the per-launch payload capabilities. The original intention was to compensate for this lower payload by lowering the per-launch costs and a high launch frequency. However, the actual costs of a Space Shuttle launch were higher than initially predicted, and the Space Shuttle did not fly the intended 24 missions per year as initially predicted by NASA.<sup id="cite_ref-griffin_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-griffin-55">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–489–490">: III–489–490 </span></sup>
</p><p>The Space Shuttle was originally intended as a launch vehicle to deploy satellites, which it was primarily used for on the missions prior to the <i>Challenger</i> disaster. NASA's pricing, which was below cost, was lower than expendable launch vehicles; the intention was that the high volume of Space Shuttle missions would compensate for early financial losses. The improvement of expendable launch vehicles and the transition away from commercial payloads on the Space Shuttle resulted in expendable launch vehicles becoming the primary deployment option for satellites.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III–109–112">: III–109–112 </span></sup> A key customer for the Space Shuttle was the <a href="/wiki/National_Reconnaissance_Office" title="National Reconnaissance Office">National Reconnaissance Office</a> (NRO) responsible for spy satellites. The existence of NRO's connection was classified through 1993, and secret considerations of NRO payload requirements led to lack of transparency in the program. The proposed <a href="/wiki/Shuttle-Centaur" title="Shuttle-Centaur">Shuttle-Centaur</a> program, cancelled in the wake of the <i>Challenger</i> disaster, would have pushed the spacecraft beyond its operational capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fatal <i>Challenger</i> and <i>Columbia</i> disasters demonstrated the safety risks of the Space Shuttle that could result in the loss of the crew. The spaceplane design of the orbiter limited the abort options, as the abort scenarios required the controlled flight of the orbiter to a runway or to allow the crew to egress individually, rather than the abort escape options on the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_(spacecraft)" title="Apollo (spacecraft)">Apollo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> space capsules.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_safety_comparison_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_safety_comparison-57">[54]</a></sup> Early safety analyses advertised by NASA engineers and management predicted the chance of a catastrophic failure resulting in the death of the crew as ranging from 1 in 100 launches to as rare as 1 in 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-ieee_challenger_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieee_challenger-58">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-feynman_appendix_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feynman_appendix-59">[56]</a></sup> Following the loss of two Space Shuttle missions, the risks for the initial missions were reevaluated, and the chance of a catastrophic loss of the vehicle and crew was found to be as high as 1 in 9.<sup id="cite_ref-npr_safety_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr_safety-60">[57]</a></sup> NASA management was criticized afterwards for accepting increased risk to the crew in exchange for higher mission rates. Both the <i><a href="/wiki/Rogers_Commission_Report" title="Rogers Commission Report">Challenger</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board" title="Columbia Accident Investigation Board">Columbia</a> </i> reports explained that NASA culture had failed to keep the crew safe by not objectively evaluating the potential risks of the missions.<sup id="cite_ref-feynman_appendix_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feynman_appendix-59">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-columbia_report_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-columbia_report-61">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 195–203">: 195–203 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Retirement"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_retirement" title="Space Shuttle retirement">Space Shuttle retirement</a></div>
<p>The Space Shuttle retirement was announced in January 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-347">: III-347 </span></sup>  President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> announced his <a href="/wiki/Vision_for_Space_Exploration" title="Vision for Space Exploration">Vision for Space Exploration</a>, which called for the retirement of the Space Shuttle once it completed construction of the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-vision_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vision-62">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bush_speech_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bush_speech-63">[60]</a></sup> To ensure the ISS was properly assembled, the contributing partners determined the need for 16 remaining assembly missions in March 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-349">: III-349 </span></sup> One additional Hubble Space Telescope servicing mission was approved in October 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-352">: III-352 </span></sup> Originally, <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a> was to be the final Space Shuttle mission. However, the <i>Columbia</i> disaster resulted in additional orbiters being prepared for <a href="/wiki/STS-3xx" title="STS-3xx">launch on need</a> in the event of a rescue mission. As <i>Atlantis</i> was prepared for the final launch-on-need mission, the decision was made in September 2010 that it would fly as <a href="/wiki/STS-135" title="STS-135">STS-135</a> with a four-person crew that could remain at the ISS in the event of an emergency.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-355">: III-355 </span></sup> STS-135 launched on July 8, 2011, and landed at the KSC on July 21, 2011, at 5:57&nbsp;a.m.&nbsp;EDT (09:57&nbsp;UTC).<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-398">: III-398 </span></sup> From then until the launch of <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_2#Crew_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon 2">Crew Dragon</a> Demo-2 on May 30, 2020, the US launched its astronauts aboard Russian Soyuz spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_crewdragon_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_crewdragon-64">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following each orbiter's final flight, it was processed to make it safe for display. The OMS and RCS systems used presented the primary dangers due to their toxic <a href="/wiki/Hypergolic_propellant" title="Hypergolic propellant">hypergolic propellant</a>, and most of their components were permanently removed to prevent any dangerous outgassing.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-443">: III-443 </span></sup> <i>Atlantis</i> is on display at the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Visitor_Complex" title="Kennedy Space Center Visitor Complex">Kennedy Space Center Visitor Complex</a> in Florida,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-456">: III-456 </span></sup> <i>Discovery</i> is on display at the <a href="/wiki/Steven_F._Udvar-Hazy_Center" title="Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center">Steven F. Udvar-Hazy Center</a> in Virginia,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-451">: III-451 </span></sup> <i>Endeavour</i> is on display at the <a href="/wiki/California_Science_Center" title="California Science Center">California Science Center</a> in Los Angeles,<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-457">: III-457 </span></sup> and <i>Enterprise</i> is displayed at the <a href="/wiki/Intrepid_Museum" title="Intrepid Museum"><i>Intrepid</i> Museum</a> in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-464">: III-464 </span></sup> Components from the orbiters were transferred to the US Air Force, ISS program, and Russian and Canadian governments. The engines were removed to be used on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Launch_System" title="Space Launch System">Space Launch System</a>, and spare RS-25 nozzles were attached for display purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins2016_21-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins2016-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: III-445">: III-445 </span></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Atlantis being greeted by a crowd after its final landing" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png/440px-Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="244" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png/660px-Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png/880px-Atlantis_welcome_home_ceremony_outside_the_OPF_July_22.png 2x" data-file-width="1639" data-file-height="908"></a><figcaption><i>Atlantis</i> after its final landing, marking the end of the Space Shuttle Program</figcaption></figure>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1217336898">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In this case, the number of successes is determined by the number of successful Space Shuttle missions.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/STS-2" title="STS-2">STS-2</a> were the only Space Shuttle missions that used a white fire-retardant coating on the external tank. Subsequent missions did not use the latex coating to reduce the mass, and the external tank appeared orange.<sup id="cite_ref-shuttle_manual_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shuttle_manual-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48">: 48 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A roll reversal is a maneuver where the bank angle is altered from one side to another. They are used to control the deviation of the azimuth from the prograde vector that results from using high bank angles to create drag.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: References"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
